<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Domestication</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Selective breeding of plants and animals to serve humans</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg/250px-Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg/375px-Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg/500px-Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Backing_sheep_at_sheepdog_competition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">Dogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> were among the first animals to be domesticated.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Domestication</b> is a sustained multi-generational relationship in  which humans assume a significant degree of control over the <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a> and care of another group of organisms to secure a more predictable supply of resources from that group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2015_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2015-1">[1]</a></sup> 
The domestication of plants and animals was a major cultural innovation ranked in importance with the conquest of fire, the manufacturing of tools, and the development of verbal language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McHugo2019_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-McHugo2019-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> recognized the small number of traits that made domestic species different from their wild ancestors. He was also the first to recognize the difference between conscious <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> (i.e. artificial selection) in which humans directly select for desirable traits, and unconscious selection where traits evolve as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> or from selection on other traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darwin1868_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-darwin1868-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2007_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014b_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014b-5">[5]</a></sup> There is a genetic difference between domestic and wild populations. There is also such a difference between the domestication traits that researchers believe to have been essential at the early stages of domestication, and the improvement traits that have appeared since the split between wild and domestic populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olsen2013_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-olsen2013-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doust2014_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-doust2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup> Domestication traits are generally fixed within all domesticates, and were selected during the initial episode of domestication of that animal or plant, whereas improvement traits are present only in a proportion of domesticates, though they may be fixed in individual breeds or <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">regional populations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doust2014_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-doust2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer2013_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-meyer2013-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestication_of_dogs" title="Domestication of dogs">dog</a> was the first domesticated <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson2012-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perri2016_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-perri2016-12">[12]</a></sup> and was established across <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> before the end of the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> era, well before <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivation</a> and before the domestication of other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2012_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-larson2012-11">[11]</a></sup> The archaeological and genetic data suggest that long-term bidirectional <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> between wild and domestic stocks – including <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkeys</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">horses</a>, New and Old World <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camelid" title="Camelid">camelids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> – was common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marshall2014_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2014-13">[13]</a></sup> Given its importance to humans and its value as a model of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demographic</a> change, domestication has attracted scientists from <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_sciences" title="Environmental sciences">environmental sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2013_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson2013-14">[14]</a></sup>
Among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a>, the major domestic species today is the <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a>, important for meat and eggs, though economically valuable <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(bird)" title="Turkey (bird)">turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guineafowl" title="Guineafowl">guineafowl</a> and numerous other species. Birds are also widely kept as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cagebirds" title="Cagebirds">cagebirds</a>, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Songbirds" title="Songbirds">songbirds</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parrots" title="Parrots">parrots</a>. The longest established <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> domesticates are the <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bee</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">silkworm</a>. <a href="/wiki/Land_snail" title="Land snail">Land snails</a> are raised for food, while species from several phyla are kept for research, and others are bred for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_control" title="Biological control">biological control</a>.
</p><p>The domestication of plants began at least 12,000 years ago with <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bottle_gourd" title="Bottle gourd">bottle gourd</a> in Asia. <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">Agriculture developed</a> in at least 11 different centres around the world, domesticating different crops and animals.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="1512" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg/220px-Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg/330px-Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg/440px-Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sedum_rubrotinctum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Succulents" title="Succulents">Succulents</a> like this jelly bean plant (<i><a href="/wiki/Sedum_rubrotinctum" title="Sedum rubrotinctum">Sedum rubrotinctum</a></i>) need infrequent watering, making them convenient as <a href="/wiki/Houseplant" title="Houseplant">houseplants</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Domestication, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">domesticus</i></span></i>, 'belonging to the house',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> is "a sustained multi-generational, <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualistic</a> relationship in which one <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a> assumes a significant degree of influence over the <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a> and care of another organism in order to secure a more predictable supply of a resource of interest, and through which the <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">partner organism</a> gains advantage over individuals that remain outside this relationship, thereby benefitting and often increasing the <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> of both the domesticator and the target domesticate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2015_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maggioni2015_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-maggioni2015-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith2014_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith2014-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sykes2014_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-sykes2014-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2012_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2012-19">[19]</a></sup> This definition recognizes both the biological and the cultural components of the domestication process and the impacts on both humans and the domesticated animals and plants. All past definitions of domestication have included a relationship between humans with plants and animals, but their differences lay in who was considered as the lead partner in the relationship. This new definition recognizes a mutualistic relationship in which both partners gain benefits. Domestication has vastly enhanced the reproductive output of crop plants, livestock, and pets far beyond that of their wild progenitors. Domesticates have provided humans with resources that they could more predictably and securely control, move, and redistribute, which has been the advantage that had fueled a population explosion of the agro-pastoralists and their spread to all corners of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2012_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2012-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Houseplant" title="Houseplant">Houseplants</a> and ornamentals are plants domesticated primarily for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aesthetic" title="Aesthetic">aesthetic</a> enjoyment in and around the home, while those domesticated for large-scale food production are called crops. Domesticated plants deliberately altered or selected for special desirable characteristics are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultigens" title="Cultigens">cultigens</a>. Animals domesticated for home companionship are called <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">pets</a>, while those domesticated for food or work are known as <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>This <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">biological mutualism</a> is not restricted to humans with domestic crops and livestock but is well-documented in nonhuman species, especially among a number of social insect domesticators and their plant and animal domesticates, for example the <a href="/wiki/Ant%E2%80%93fungus_mutualism" title="Ant–fungus mutualism">ant–fungus mutualism</a> that exists between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leafcutter_ants" title="Leafcutter ants">leafcutter ants</a> and certain fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2015_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2015-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Domestication_syndrome" title="Domestication syndrome">Domestication syndrome</a> is the suite of <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypic</a> traits arising during domestication that distinguish crops from their wild ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olsen2013_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-olsen2013-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hammer1984_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-hammer1984-20">[20]</a></sup> The term is also applied to vertebrate animals, and includes increased docility and tameness, coat color changes, reductions in tooth size, changes in craniofacial morphology, alterations in ear and tail form (e.g., floppy ears), more frequent and nonseasonal estrus cycles, alterations in <a href="/wiki/Adrenocorticotropic_hormone" title="Adrenocorticotropic hormone">adrenocorticotropic hormone</a> levels, changed concentrations of several neurotransmitters, prolongations in juvenile behavior, and reductions in both total brain size and of particular brain regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkins2014_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilkins2014-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px
