<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause_and_timing">Cause and timing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cause and timing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_(Younger_Dryas).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="1786" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_%28Younger_Dryas%29.jpg/440px-Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_%28Younger_Dryas%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_%28Younger_Dryas%29.jpg/660px-Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_%28Younger_Dryas%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_%28Younger_Dryas%29.jpg/880px-Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_%28Younger_Dryas%29.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_temperature_in_the_Post-Glacial_period_according_to_Greenland_ice_cores_(Younger_Dryas).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of temperatures in the postglacial period, after the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a>, showing very low temperatures for the most part of the Younger Dryas, rapidly rising afterwards to reach the level of the warm <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_cores" title="Greenland ice cores">Greenland ice cores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zalloua2017_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zalloua2017-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">domestication of animals</a> and plants was triggered by the climatic and environmental changes that occurred after the peak of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> around 21,000 years ago and which continue to this present day. These changes made obtaining food difficult. The first domesticate was the <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolf</a> (<i>Canis lupus</i>) at least 15,000 years ago. The <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">Younger Dryas</a> that occurred 12,900 years ago was a period of intense cold and aridity that put pressure on humans to intensify their foraging strategies. By the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> from 11,700 years ago, favorable climatic conditions and increasing human populations led to small-scale animal and plant domestication, which allowed humans to augment the food that they were obtaining through <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gathering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McHugo2019_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-McHugo2019-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_transition" title="Neolithic transition">Neolithic transition</a> led to agricultural societies emerging in locations across Eurasia, North Africa, and South and Central America. In the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> 10,000-11,000 years ago, <a href="/wiki/Zooarchaeology" title="Zooarchaeology">zooarchaeology</a> indicates that goats, pigs, sheep, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taurine_cattle" title="Taurine cattle">taurine cattle</a> were the first livestock to be domesticated. Two thousand years later, humped <a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">zebu</a> cattle were domesticated in what is today <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baluchistan" title="Baluchistan">Baluchistan</a> in Pakistan. In <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> 8,000 years ago, pigs were domesticated from wild boar that were genetically different from those found in the Fertile Crescent. The horse was domesticated on the Central Asian steppe 5,500 years ago. Both the chicken in Southeast Asia and the cat in Egypt were domesticated 4,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McHugo2019_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-McHugo2019-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sudden appearance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_dog" title="Domestic dog">domestic dog</a> (<i>Canis lupus familiaris</i>) in the archaeological record then led to a rapid shift in the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Demography" title="Demography">demography</a> of both humans and numerous species of animals and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machugh2016_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-machugh2016-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup> It was followed by livestock and crop domestication, and the transition of humans from foraging to farming in different places and times across the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machugh2016_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-machugh2016-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller2011_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuller2011-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2006_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2006-25">[25]</a></sup> Around 10,000 YBP, a new way of life emerged for humans through the management and exploitation of plant and animal species, leading to higher-density populations in the centers of domestication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machugh2016_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-machugh2016-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bocquet2011_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-bocquet2011-26">[26]</a></sup> the expansion of agricultural economies, and the development of urban communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machugh2016_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-machugh2016-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barker2006_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-barker2006-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals"><span id="Animals"></span>Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">Domestication of animals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="375" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg/170px-Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg/255px-Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg/340px-Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harold_f_Weston_-_Iran7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Karakul_sheep" title="Karakul sheep">Karakul sheep</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[a]</a></sup> and shepherds in Iran. Photograph by Harold F. Weston, 1920s</div></div></div>
<p>The domestication of animals is the relationship between animals and humans who have influence on their "care" and reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2015_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2015-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> recognized the small number of traits that made domestic species different from their wild ancestors. He was also the first to recognize the difference between conscious <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> in which humans directly select for desirable traits, and unconscious selection where traits evolve as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> or from selection on other traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darwin1868_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-darwin1868-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2007_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014b_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014b-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a difference between domestic and wild populations, though studies suggest domestication as a form of survival for most animals under human care. There is also such a difference between the domestication traits that researchers believe to have been essential at the early stages of domestication, and the improvement traits that have appeared since the split between wild and domestic populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olsen2013_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-olsen2013-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doust2014_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-doust2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup> Domestication traits are generally fixed within all domesticates, and were selected during the initial episode of domestication of that animal or plant, whereas improvement traits are present only in a proportion of domesticates, though they may be fixed in individual breeds or <a href="/wiki/Landrace" title="Landrace">regional populations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doust2014_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-doust2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meyer2013_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-meyer2013-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Domestication</i> of animals should not be confused with <a href="/wiki/Tame_animal" title="Tame animal"><i>taming</i></a>. Taming is the conditioned behavioral modification of an individual animal, to reduce its natural avoidance of humans, and to tolerate the presence of humans. Domestication is the permanent genetic modification of a bred lineage that leads to an inherited predisposition to respond calmly to human presence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-price2008_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-price2008-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-macdonald2009_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-macdonald2009-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2012_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2012-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain animal species, and certain individuals within those species, make better candidates for domestication merely for their inability to defend themselves. These animals exhibit certain behavioral characteristics:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2012_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2012-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: Fig 1">: Fig 1 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hale1969_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-hale1969-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-price1984_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-price1984-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-price2002_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-price2002-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The size and organization of their social structure</li>
<li>The availability and the degree of selectivity in their choice of mates</li>
<li>The ease and speed with which the parents bond with their young, and the maturity and mobility of the young at birth</li>
<li>The degree of flexibility in diet and habitat tolerance; and</li>
<li>Responses to humans and new environments, including reduced flight response and reactivity to external stimuli.</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">Domestication of animals</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_animals" title="List of domesticated animals">List of domesticated animals</a></div>
<p>The beginnings of animal domestication involved a protracted <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">coevolutionary</a> process with multiple stages along different pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup> There are three proposed major pathways that most animal domesticates followed into domestication:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Commensalism" title="Commensalism">commensals</a>, adapted to a human niche (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dogs" title="Dogs">dogs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cats" title="Cats">cats</a>, possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigs" title="Pigs">pigs</a>);</li>
<li>prey animals sought for food (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goats" title="Goats">goats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_yak" title="Domestic yak">yak</a>, pig, <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpaca</a>); and</li>
<li>animals targeted for draft and non-food resources (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marshall2014_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2014-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2012_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2012-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frantz2015b_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-frantz2015b-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blaustein2015_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-blaustein2015-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vahabi2015_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-vahabi2015-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gepts2012_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-gepts2012-39">[38]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>The dog was the first domesticant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2012_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-larson2012-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perri2016_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-perri2016-12">[12]</a></sup> and was established across <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> before the end of the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> era, well before <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">cultivation</a> and before the domestication of other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2012_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-larson2012-11">[11]</a></sup> Humans did not intend to domesticate animals from either the commensal or prey pathways, or at least they did not envision a domesticated animal would result from it. In both of those cases, humans became entangled with these species as the relationship between them intensified, and humans' role in their survival and reproduction led gradually to formalised <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup> Although the directed pathway proceeded from capture to taming, the other two pathways are not as goal-oriented, and archaeological records suggest that they took place over much longer time frames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2013_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-larson2013-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike other domestic species which were primarily selected for production-related traits, dogs were initially selected for their behaviors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serpell2014_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-serpell2014-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cagan2016_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-cagan2016-41">[40]</a></sup> The archaeological and genetic data suggest that long-term bidirectional <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> between wild and domestic stocks – including <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkeys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a>, New and Old World <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camelid" title="Camelid">camelids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> – was common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson2014_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-larson2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marshall2014_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-marshall2014-13">[13]</a></sup> One study has concluded that human selection for domestic traits likely counteracted the homogenizing effect of gene flow from wild boars into pigs and created <a href="/wiki/Domestication_islands" title="Domestication islands">domestication islands</a> in the genome. The same process may also apply to other domesticated animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frantz2015_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-frantz2015-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pennisi2015_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-pennisi2015-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Insects"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_Junglefowl_(male)_-_Thailand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="742" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Red_Junglefowl_%28male%29_-_Thailand.jpg/220px-Red_Junglefowl_%28male%29_-_Thailand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Red_Junglefowl_%28male%29_-_Thailand.jpg/330px-Red_Junglefowl_%28male%29_-_Thailand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Red_Junglefowl_%28male%29_-_Thailand.jpg/440px-Red_Junglefowl_%28male%29_-_Thailand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_Junglefowl_(male)_-_Thailand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Red_junglefowl" title="Red junglefowl">red junglefowl</a> of Southeast Asia was domesticated, apparently for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockfighting" title="Cockfighting">cockfighting</a>, some 7,000 years ago.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">Poultry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aviculture" title="Aviculture">Aviculture</a></div>
<p>Domesticated birds principally mean <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>, raised for meat and eggs:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AH_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-AH-44">[43]</a></sup> some <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">Galliformes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(bird)" title="Turkey (bird)">turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guineafowl" title="Guineafowl">guineafowl</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Anseriformes" title="Anseriformes">Anseriformes</a> (waterfowl: <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">duck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goose" title="Goose">goose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swan</a>). Also widely domesticated are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cagebirds" title="Cagebirds">cagebirds</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Songbirds" title="Songbirds">songbirds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parrots" title="Parrots">parrots</a>; these are kept both for pleasure and for use in research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Domestic_pigeon" title="Domestic pigeon">domestic pigeon</a> has been used both for food and as a means of communication between far-flung places through the exploitation of the pigeon's homing instinct; research suggests it was domesticated as early as 10,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blechman_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blechman-46">[45]</a></sup>
Chicken fossils in China were dated 7,400 years ago. The chicken's wild ancestor is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallus_gallus" title="Gallus gallus">Gallus gallus</a></i>, the red junglefowl of Southeast Asia. It appears to have been kept initially for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cockfighting" title="Cockfighting">cockfighting</a> rather than for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invertebrates">Invertebrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Invertebrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestication_of_bees" title="Domestication of bees">Domestication of bees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">Beekeeping</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">Sericulture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sericuturist.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="284" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Sericuturist.jpg/170px-Sericuturist.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Sericuturist.jpg/255px-Sericuturist.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Sericuturist.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sericuturist.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">Sericulturalists</a> preparing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">silkworms</a> for spinning of the <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a></div></div></div>
<p>Two <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">silkworm</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_honey_bee" title="Western honey bee">western honey bee</a>, have been domesticated for over 5,000 years, often for commercial use. The silkworm is raised for the silk threads wound around its <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupal</a> cocoon; the western honey bee, for <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, and, lately, for <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a> of crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several other invertebrates have been domesticated, both terrestrial and aquatic, including some such as <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">Drosophila melanogaster</a></i> fruit flies and the freshwater cnidarian <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydra_(animal)" title="Hydra (animal)">Hydra</a></i> for research into genetics and physiology. Few have a long history of domestication. Most are used for food or other products such as <a href="/wiki/Shellac" title="Shellac">shellac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cochineal" title="Cochineal">cochineal</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> involved are <a href="/wiki/Cnidaria" title="Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platyhelminthes" title="Platyhelminthes">Platyhelminthes</a> (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_control" title="Biological control">biological control</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annelida" title="Annelida">Annelida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">Mollusca</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthropoda" title="Arthropoda">Arthropoda</a> (marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">crustaceans</a> as well as insects and spiders), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echinodermata" title="Echinodermata">Echinodermata</a>. While many marine molluscs are used for food, only a few have been domesticated, including <a href="/wiki/Loligo" title="Loligo">squid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepia_(genus)" title="Sepia (genus)">cuttlefish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopus</a>, all used in research on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_behaviour" title="Animal behaviour">behaviour</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">neurology</a>. Terrestrial snails in the genera <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helix_(genus)" title="Helix (genus)">Helix</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Murex" title="Murex">Murex</a></i> are raised for food. Several parasitic or parasitoidal insects including the fly <i><a href="/wiki/Eucelatoria" title="Eucelatoria">Eucelatoria</a></i>, the beetle <i><a href="/wiki/Chrysolina" title="Chrysolina">Chrysolina</a></i>, and the wasp <i><a href="/wiki/Aphytis_(insect)" title="Aphytis (insect)">Aphytis</a></i> are raised for biological control. Conscious or unconscious artificial selection has many effects on species under domestication; variability can readily be lost by inbreeding, selection against undesired traits, or genetic drift, while in <i>Drosophila</i>, variability in eclosion time (when adults emerge) has increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GonPrice1984_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-GonPrice1984-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Plants"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_plants" title="List of domesticated plants">List of domesticated plants</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The initial domestication of animals impacted most on the genes that controlled their behavior, but the initial domestication of plants impacted most on the genes that controlled their morphology (seed size, plant architecture, dispersal mechanisms) and their physiology (timing of germination or ripening).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2012_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2012-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeder2006_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-zeder2006-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The domestication of <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> provides an example. Wild wheat <a href="/wiki/Shattering_(agriculture)" title="Shattering (agriculture)">shatters</a> and falls to the ground to reseed itself when ripe, but domesticated wheat stays on the stem for easier harvesting. This change was possible because of a random mutation in the wild populations at the beginning of wheat's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_cultivation" title="Plant cultivation">cultivation</a>. Wheat with this mutation was harvested more frequently and became the seed for the next crop. Therefore, without realizing, early farmers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_selection" title="Artificial selection">selected for</a> this mutation. The result is domesticated wheat, which relies on farmers for its reproduction and dissemination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg/220px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg/330px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg/440px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farmers with wheat and cattle – Ancient Egyptian art 3,400 years ago</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest human attempts at plant domestication occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. There is early evidence for conscious cultivation and trait selection of plants by pre-Neolithic groups in Syria: grains of <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> with domestic traits dated 13,000 years ago have been recovered from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hureyra" title="Abu Hureyra">Abu Hureyra</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillman_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillman-51">[50]</a></sup> but this appears to be a localised phenomenon resulting from cultivation of stands of wild rye, rather than a definitive step towards domestication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillman_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hillman-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bottle_gourd" title="Bottle gourd">bottle gourd</a> (<i>Lagenaria siceraria</i>) plant, used as a container before the advent of <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> technology, appears to have been domesticated 10,000 years ago. The domesticated bottle gourd reached the Americas from Asia by 8,000 years ago, most likely due to the migration of peoples from Asia to America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cereal crops were first domesticated around 11,000 years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> in the Middle East. The first domesticated crops were generally annuals with large seeds or fruits. These included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulse_(legume)" title="Pulse (legume)">pulses</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pea" title="Pea">peas</a> and grains such as <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>. The Middle East was especially suited to these species; the dry-summer climate was conducive to the evolution of large-seeded annual plants, and the variety of elevations led to a great variety of species. As domestication took place humans began to move from a <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> society to a settled <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a> society. This change would eventually lead, some 4000 to 5000 years later, to the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_state" title="City state">city states</a> and eventually the rise of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> itself.
</p><p>Continued domestication was gradual, a process of intermittent trial and error, and often resulted in diverging traits and characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Over time perennials and small trees including the <a href="/wiki/Apple" title="Apple">apple</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olive</a> were domesticated. Some plants, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macadamia_nut" title="Macadamia nut">macadamia nut</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecan</a>, were not domesticated until recently.
</p><p>In other parts of the world very different species were domesticated. In the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(fruit)" title="Squash (fruit)">squash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beans" title="Beans">beans</a>, and perhaps <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>) formed the core of the diet. In East Asia <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">soy</a> were the most important crops. Some areas of the world such as <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cone" title="Southern Cone">southern South America</a> never saw local species domesticated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_from_wild_plants">Differences from wild plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Differences from wild plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Domesticated plants may differ from their wild relatives in many ways, including
</p>
<ul><li>the way they spread to a more diverse environment and have a wider geographic range;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeven_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeven-54">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>different ecological preference (sun, water, temperature, nutrients, etc. requirements), different disease susceptibility;</li>
<li>conversion from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a> to annual;</li>
<li>loss of <a href="/wiki/Seed_dormancy" title="Seed dormancy">seed dormancy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photoperiodism" title="Photoperiodism">photoperiodic</a> controls;</li>
<li>simultaneous flower and fruit, double flowers;</li>
<li>a lack of <a href="/wiki/Shattering_(agriculture)" title="Shattering (agriculture)">shattering</a> or scattering of seeds, or even loss of their dispersal mechanisms completely;</li>
<li>less efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breeding_system" title="Breeding system">breeding system</a> (e.g. lack normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pollinating" title="Pollinating">pollinating</a> organs, making human intervention a requirement), smaller seeds with lower success in the wild, or even complete sexual sterility (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Fruit#Seedless_fruits" title="Fruit">seedless fruits</a>) and therefore only vegetative reproduction;</li>
<li>less defensive adaptations such as hairs, <a href="/wiki/Thorns,_spines,_and_prickles" title="Thorns, spines, and prickles">thorns, spines, and prickles</a>, poison, protective coverings and sturdiness, rendering them more likely to be eaten by animals and pests unless cared by humans;</li>
<li>chemical composition, giving them better <a href="/wiki/Palatability" title="Palatability">palatability</a> (e.g. sugar content), better smell, and lower toxicity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>edible part larger, and easier separated from non-edible part (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Drupe" title="Drupe">freestone fruit</a>).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_impact_of_domestication_on_the_plant_microbiome">The impact of domestication on the plant microbiome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The impact of domestication on the plant microbiome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:349px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_impact_of_plant_evolutions,_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3579" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_impact_of_plant_evolutions%2C_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg/347px-The_impact_of_plant_evolutions%2C_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_impact_of_plant_evolutions%2C_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg/521px-The_impact_of_plant_evolutions%2C_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_impact_of_plant_evolutions%2C_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg/694px-The_impact_of_plant_evolutions%2C_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg 2x" width="347"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_impact_of_plant_evolutions,_domestication_and_breeding_on_the_plant_microbiome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A conceptual figure on the impact of domestication on the plant endophytic microbiome. (a) A phylogenetic distance among <i>Malus</i> species which contains wild species (black branches) and progenitor wild species (blue branches). The extended green branch represents <i>Malus domestica</i> with its close affiliation its main ancestor (<i>M. sieversii</i>). Dashed lines indicate introgression events between <i>Malus</i> progenitors which contributed to the formation of <i>M. domestica</i>. (b) The predicted three scenarios: Scenario 1, reduction in species diversity due to loss in microbial species; Scenario 2, increase in microbial diversity due to introgressive hybridization during the apple domestication; Scenario 3, diversity was not affected by domestication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-56">[55]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Microbiome" title="Microbiome">microbiome</a>, defined as the collection of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> inhabiting the surface and internal tissue of plants, has been shown to be affected by plant domestication and breeding. This includes variation the microbial community composition <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-56">[55]</a></sup> to change in the number of microbial species associated with plants, i.e., species diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-56">[55]</a></sup> Evidence also show that plant lineage, including <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>, domestication, and <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">breeding</a> have shaped the plant <a href="/wiki/Endophyte" title="Endophyte">endophytes</a> in similar patterns as plant genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-:4-56">[55]</a></sup>  Such patterns are also known as <a href="/wiki/Phylosymbiosis" title="Phylosymbiosis">phylosymbiosis</a> which have been observed in several animal and plant lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traits_that_are_being_genetically_improved">Traits that are being genetically improved</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Traits that are being genetically improved">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are many challenges facing modern farmers, including climate change, pests, soil salinity, drought, and periods with limited sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Drought is one of the most serious challenges facing farmers today. With shifting climates comes shifting weather patterns, meaning that regions that could traditionally rely on a substantial amount of precipitation were, quite literally, left out to dry. In light of these conditions, <a href="/wiki/Drought_tolerance" title="Drought tolerance">drought resistance</a> in major crop plants has become a clear priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> One method is to identify the genetic basis of drought resistance in naturally drought resistant plants, i.e. the <a href="/wiki/Vigna_subterranea" title="Vigna subterranea">Bambara groundnut</a>. Next, transferring these advantages to otherwise vulnerable crop plants. Rice, which is one of the most vulnerable crops in terms of drought, has been successfully improved by the addition of the Barley hva1 gene into the genome using transgenetics. Drought resistance can also be improved through changes in a plant's root system architecture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> such as a root orientation that maximizes water retention and nutrient uptake. There must be a continued focus on the efficient usage of available water on a planet that is expected to have a population in excess of nine-billion people by 2050.
</p><p>Another specific area of genetic improvement for domesticated crops is the crop plant's uptake and utilization of soil potassium, an essential element for crop plants yield and overall quality. A plant's ability to effectively uptake potassium and utilize it efficiently is known as its potassium utilization efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> It has been suggested that first optimizing plant root architecture and then root potassium uptake activity may effectively improve plant potassium utilization efficiency.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_plants_that_are_being_genetically_improved">Crop plants that are being genetically improved</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Crop plants that are being genetically improved">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">Cereals</a>, rice, wheat, corn, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a> and barley, make up a huge amount of the global diet across all demographic and social scales. These cereal crop plants are all autogamous, i.e. self-fertilizing, which limits overall diversity in allelic combinations, and therefore adaptability to novel environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> To combat this issue the researchers suggest an "Island Model of Genomic Selection". By breaking a single large population of cereal crop plants into several smaller sub-populations which can receive "migrants" from the other subpopulations, new genetic combinations can be generated.
</p><p>The Bambara groundnut is a durable crop plant that, like many underutilized crops, has received little attention in an agricultural sense. The Bambara Groundnut is drought resistant and is known to be able to grow in almost any soil conditions, no matter how impoverished an area may be. New genomic and transcriptomic approaches are allowing researchers to improve this relatively small-scale crop, as well as other large-scale crop plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-68">[67]</a></sup> The reduction in cost, and wide availability of both <a href="/wiki/Microarray" title="Microarray">microarray</a> technology and Next Generation Sequencing have made it possible to analyze underutilized crops, like the groundnut, at genome-wide level. Not overlooking particular crops that don't appear to hold any value outside of the developing world will be key to not only overall crop improvement, but also to reducing the global dependency on only a few crop plants, which holds many intrinsic dangers to the global population's food supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges_facing_genetic_improvement">Challenges facing genetic improvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Challenges facing genetic improvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The semi-arid tropics, ranging from parts of North and South Africa, Asia especially in the South Pacific, all the way to Australia are notorious for being both economically destitute and agriculturally difficult to cultivate and farm effectively. Barriers include everything from lack of rainfall and diseases, to economic isolation and environmental irresponsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> There is a large interest in the continued efforts, of the <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics</a> (ICRSAT) to improve staple foods. some mandated crops of ICRISAT include the groundnut, pigeonpea, chickpea, sorghum and pearl millet, which are the main staple foods for nearly one billion people in the semi-arid tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-70">[69]</a></sup> As part of the ICRISAT efforts, some wild plant breeds are being used to transfer genes to cultivated crops by interspecific hybridization involving modern methods of embryo rescue and tissue culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> One example of early success has been work to combat the very detrimental <a href="/wiki/Peanut_clump_virus" title="Peanut clump virus">peanut clump virus</a>. Transgenetic plants containing the coat protein gene for resistance against peanut clump virus have already been produced successfully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-70">[69]</a></sup> Another region threatened by food security are the Pacific Island Countries, which are disproportionally faced with the negative effects of climate change. The Pacific Islands are largely made up of a chain of small bodies of land, which obviously limits the amount of geographical area in which to farm. This leaves the region with only two viable options 1.) increase agricultural production or 2.) increase food importation. The latter of course runs into the issues of availability and economic feasibility, leaving only the first option as a viable means to solve the region's food crisis.  It is much easier to misuse the limited resources remaining, as compared with solving the problem at its core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_with_wild_plants_to_improve_domestics">Working with wild plants to improve domestics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Working with wild plants to improve domestics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Work has also has been focusing on improving domestic crops through the use of <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relative" title="Crop wild relative">crop wild relatives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-70">[69]</a></sup> The amount and depth of genetic material available in crop wild relatives is larger than originally believed, and the range of plants involved, both wild and domestic, is ever expanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-73">[72]</a></sup> Through the use of new biotechnological tools such as genome editing, <a href="/wiki/Cisgenesis" title="Cisgenesis">cisgenesis</a>/intragenesis, the transfer of genes between crossable donor species including hybrids, and other omic approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wild plants can be hybridized with crop plants to form perennial crops from annuals, increase yield, growth rate, and resistance to outside pressures like disease and drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-74">[73]</a></sup> Importantly, these changes take significant lengths of time to achieve, sometimes even decades. However, the outcome can be extremely successful as is the case with a hybrid grass variant known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernza" title="Kernza">Kernza</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-74">[73]</a></sup> Over the course of nearly three decades, work was done on an attempted hybridization between an already domesticated grass strain, and several of its wild relatives. The domesticated strain as was more uniform in its orientation, but the wild strains were larger and propagated faster. The resulting <i>Kernza</i> crop has traits from both progenitors: uniform orientation and a linearly vertical root system from the domesticated crop, along with increased size and rate of propagation from the wild relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungi_and_micro-organisms">Fungi and micro-organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fungi and micro-organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_fungi_and_microorganisms" title="List of domesticated fungi and microorganisms">List of domesticated fungi and microorganisms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Champignons_Agaricus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Champignons_Agaricus.jpg/220px-Champignons_Agaricus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Champignons_Agaricus.jpg/330px-Champignons_Agaricus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Champignons_Agaricus.jpg/440px-Champignons_Agaricus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Champignons_Agaricus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Agaricus_bisporus" title="Agaricus bisporus">Button mushrooms</a> are widely cultivated for food.</div></div></div>
<p>Several species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> have been domesticated for use directly as food, or in fermentation to produce foods and drugs. The white button mushroom <i><a href="/wiki/Agaricus_bisporus" title="Agaricus bisporus">Agaricus bisporus</a></i> is widely grown for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The yeast <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i> have been used for thousands of years to ferment beer and wine, and to leaven bread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> Mould fungi including <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i> are used to mature cheeses and other dairy products, as well as to make drugs such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_domestic_animals">On domestic animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: On domestic animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Selection of animals for visible "desirable" traits may have undesired consequences. Captive and domesticated animals often have smaller size, <a href="/wiki/Piebald" title="Piebald">piebald</a> color, shorter faces with smaller and fewer teeth, diminished horns, weak muscle ridges, and less genetic variability.  Poor joint definition, late fusion of the limb bone <a href="/wiki/Epiphysis" title="Epiphysis">epiphyses</a> with the diaphyses, hair changes, greater fat accumulation, smaller brains, simplified behavior patterns, extended immaturity, and more pathology are among the defects of domestic animals.  All of these changes have been documented by archaeological evidence, and confirmed by animal breeders in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> In 2014, a study proposed the theory that under selection, docility in mammals and birds results partly from a slowed pace of <a href="/wiki/Neural_crest" title="Neural crest">neural crest</a> development, that would in turn cause a reduced fear–startle response due to mild <a href="/wiki/Neurocristopathy" title="Neurocristopathy">neurocristopathy</a> that causes domestication syndrome. The theory was unable to explain curly tails nor domestication syndrome exhibited by plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilkins2014_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-wilkins2014-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>A side effect of domestication has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoonotic" title="Zoonotic">zoonotic</a> diseases. For example, cattle have given humanity various <a href="/wiki/Poxviridae" title="Poxviridae">viral poxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>; pigs and ducks have given <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>; and horses have given the <a href="/wiki/Rhinovirus" title="Rhinovirus">rhinoviruses</a>. Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> have their origins in domestic animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2007_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The advent of domestication resulted in denser human populations which provided ripe conditions for pathogens to reproduce, mutate, spread, and eventually find a new host in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Shepard" title="Paul Shepard">Paul Shepard</a> writes "Man substitutes controlled breeding for natural selection; animals are selected for special traits like milk production or passivity, at the expense of overall <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> and nature-wide relationships...Though domestication broadens the diversity of forms – that is, increases visible polymorphism – it undermines the crisp demarcations that separate wild species and cripples our recognition of the species as a group. Knowing only domestic animals dulls our understanding of the way in which unity and discontinuity occur as patterns in nature, and substitutes an attention to individuals and breeds. The wide variety of size, color, shape, and form of domestic horses, for example, blurs the distinction among different species of <i>Equus</i> that once were constant and meaningful."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_society">On society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: On society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jared_Diamond" title="Jared Diamond">Jared Diamond</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Guns,_Germs,_and_Steel" title="Guns, Germs, and Steel">Guns, Germs, and Steel</a></i> describes the universal tendency for populations that have acquired agriculture and domestic animals to develop a large population and to expand into new territories. He recounts migrations of people armed with domestic crops overtaking, displacing or killing indigenous hunter-gatherers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2007_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 112">: 112 </span></sup> whose lifestyle is coming to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diamond2007_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-diamond2007-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 86">: 86 </span></sup>
</p><p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivist" title="Anarcho-primitivist">anarcho-primitivist</a> authors describe domestication as the process by which previously <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomadic" title="Nomadic">nomadic</a> human populations shifted towards a sedentary or settled existence through <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>. They claim that this kind of domestication demands a totalitarian relationship with both the land and the plants and animals being domesticated. They say that whereas, in a state of wildness, all life shares and competes for resources, domestication destroys this balance. Domesticated landscape (e.g. pastoral lands/agricultural fields and, to a lesser degree, <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gardening" title="Gardening">gardening</a>) ends the open sharing of resources; where "this was everyone's", it is now "mine". Anarcho-primitivists state that this notion of ownership laid the foundation for social <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> as property and power emerged. It also involved the destruction, enslavement, or assimilation of other groups of early people who did not make such a transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the framework of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialectical_naturalism" title="Dialectical naturalism">Dialectical naturalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Murray_Bookchin" title="Murray Bookchin">Murray Bookchin</a> has argued that the basic notion of domestication is incomplete: That, since the domestication of animals is a crucial development within human history, it can also be understood as the domestication of humanity itself in turn. Under this dialectical framework, domestication is always a 'two-way street' with both parties being unavoidably altered by their relationship with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Nibert" title="David Nibert">David Nibert</a>, professor of sociology at <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg_University" title="Wittenberg University">Wittenberg University</a>, posits that the domestication of animals, which he refers to as "domesecration" as it often involved extreme violence against animal populations and the devastation of the environment, resulted in the corruption of human ethics, and helped pave the way for societies steeped in "conquest, extermination, displacement, repression, coerced and enslaved servitude, gender subordination and sexual exploitation, and hunger."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_diversity">On diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: On diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_harvest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wheat_harvest.jpg/220px-Wheat_harvest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wheat_harvest.jpg/330px-Wheat_harvest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Wheat_harvest.jpg/440px-Wheat_harvest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_harvest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Industrialized wheat harvest – North America today</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">Sustainable agriculture</a></div>
<p>In 2016, a study found that humans have had a major impact on global <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> as well as extinction rates, including a contribution to megafaunal extinctions. Pristine landscapes no longer exist and have not existed for millennia, and humans have concentrated the planet's biomass into human-favored plants and animals. Domesticated ecosystems provide food, reduce predator and natural dangers, and promote commerce, but have also resulted in habitat loss and extinctions commencing in the Late Pleistocene. Ecologists and other researchers are advised to make better use of the archaeological and paleoecological data available for gaining an understanding the history of human impacts before proposing solutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boivin2016_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-boivin2016-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">

</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Domestication&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
